#### Raw script
```
Instructional Notes
The Getting Ready lessons provide suggested
explanations for each sound and its place
and manner of articulation. This is merely an
introduction to the sound wall and the sounds
within the English language. Each of these sounds
and their spelling patterns will be explicitly
introduced and practiced within the main lessons
of the curriculum.
This initial lesson activity does not require any
materials or preparation. Children should be
seated with a clear view of the teacher's mouth for
modeling purposes.
Introduction: Sounds
When we speak, we make sounds.
Say this sound: /mmmm/. (/mmmm/). We use
/mmm/ when we say "mouse."
Now say this sound: /ssss/. (/ssss/). We use /sss/
when we say "sun."
Now try this one: /GGaG/. (/4646/). We use /44G/
when we say "apple."
How about this one: /p/. (/p/). That one was
different. We can't hold out /p/ like we did with
/sss/ or /44G/. We use /p/ when we say "pig."
We use our mouths and all the parts inside it to
make sounds.
Continued from previous column...
We use our lips: /mmm/.../p/. We use our tongues:
/t/.../k/. And we use our teeth: /fff/.../thhh/.
Stopped & Continuous Sounds
We also sometimes use our nose. Try this. Say
/nnn/ and now hold your nose. (/nnn/). What
happened? The sound stopped. A
lot goes into
making the sounds we say when we speak.
Voiced & Unvoiced Sounds
Another part we use when we speak is our vocal
cords. Vocal cords are in our throat, and they
vibrate and make sounds (model the sound from
vibration with a guitar string or thick rubber band,
if possible). You can tell if your vocal cords are
vibrating by putting your hand on your neck like
this (model).
Say each sound
| say and feel to see if your vocal
cords are vibrating. If they are, nod yes. If they
aren't, shake your head no. Ready?: /mmm/ (yes),
/sss/ (no), /AGG/ (yes), /shh/ (no), /nnn/ (yes),
/ffF/ (no).
If a sound vibrates when we say it, we say our
voices are on. When we say these sounds, our
voices are on: /mmm/, /aaa/, /vvv/, /nnn/. If the
sound doesn't vibrate, we say our voices are off.
When we say these sounds, our voices are off:
/sss/, /fff/, /shhh/.
Alphabet Knowledge & Letter Formation
Instructional Notes
The Getting Ready lessons cover uppercase
letter formation. Lowercase letter formation is
explicitly taught in the alphabet unit of the phonics
lessons. It is important to note that 1st and 2nd
graders who skip these lessons and the alphabet
unit will not get explicit uppercase or lowercase
letter formation instruction. Those that struggle
with letter formation may need additional letter
formation support.
Introduction
Today we will learn how to sky write, trace, and
practice making circles and curves. This will help
us write some letters of the alphabet.
Sky Writing
Sky writing is a way for us to practice shapes using
big movements. We use the hand we write with
to sky write. Have students stand up to practice.
Everyone raise the hand you write with. This is
your writing arm. Hold your writing arm out in
front of you and point your writing finger. Now put
your other hand on the shoulder of your writing
arm. Now move your writing arm up and down and
side to side. This is sky writing.
Continued on next page...
6 ieee
Ee eee)

Curves
Tracing is a way for us to practice shapes using
A curve is a circle that does not go all the way
small movements. When we trace we use our
around. We can make curves that look like half
finger to make a shape. We use the hand we write
circles, smiles, and humps which look like little
with to trace. Hold out the hand you write with
hills/bumps.
and touch your index finger to your desk or the

and forth across the table or the carpet. This is
tracing.

Start at the top. Curve this way (counterclockwise).

try skywriting a circle. Have students stand up to

making more circles in a variety of sizes (big and
small) and on a variety of surfaces (the wall, a
tabletop, the floor, their palm, etc.).

#### Raw script
```
Instructional Notes
All sounds can be categorized as consonants
or vowels. From an articulation standpoint, the
distinction between consonants and vowels has to
do with airflow. Consonant sounds are produced by
disrupting airflow in some way. Consonant sounds
can be voiced or unvoiced.
For this lesson, the teacher will need to have the
column headers for the consonant grid already
posted (Lips Together, Teeth on Lip, Tongue
Between Teeth, Tongue Behind Top Teeth, Lips
Rounded/Tongue Back, Back of Tongue Lifted, and
Back of Throat).
Students should be seated in an area where
they can clearly see the consonant grid and the
teacher's mouth.
Introduction: Place of Articulation
Consonant sounds are made by moving the parts
of your mouth, like your teeth, lips, or tongue,
usually so thot one part of your mouth touches
another part. (Point to those headers on the
consonant grid.) When we move our mouth parts
to make consonant sounds, we also block air from
flowing out of our mouths. Sometimes we block it
all the way, and sometimes we only block it part
of the way. For some consonant sounds, we'll turn
our voices on. For others, we won't.
We're going to organize all the consonant sounds
ona Sound Wall. (Point to the sound wall.) We'll
organize them by which parts of our mouth are used
in making the sound (point to the column headers),
what we do as we make the sound, and whether our
voice is on or off when we make the sound.
Today, we will be talking about which parts of our
mouth we use to make different sounds. There are
seven places where we can make sounds.
Lips Together
The first is with our lips together (model and point
to picture). See my lips are closed. Do you know
any sounds we can make with our lips together?
(Wait for responses.) Yes, those are some sounds
46
Continued from previous column...
we make with our lips together. (Provide any sounds

/p/, /b/, /m/. You try (model again, as needed).

teeth on our lips (model and point to picture). See
my bottom lip is touching my top teeth. Do you
know any sounds we can make with our bottom lip
touching our top teeth? (Wait for responses.) Yes,
those are some sounds we make with our teeth on
our lips. (Provide any sounds that the students did
not state.) Here are some more: /f/, /v/. You try
(model again, as needed).

[z], [\J. You try (model again, as needed).
Lips Rounded, Tongue Back

rounded and our tongue back (model and point to
picture). See my lips are rounded, and the middle
part of my tongue touches the roof of my mouth.

can make with our tongue pulled back?
Continued from previous column...

we make with our tongue pulled back. (Provide
any sounds that the students did not state.) Here
are some more: /k/, /g/, /n/, /w/. You try (model
again, as needed).
Back of Throat
The seventh and last place we can make sounds is in
the back of our throat (model and point to picture).
My tongue pulls back and almost blocks the back of
my throat. Do you know the'one sound we can make
in the back of our throats? (Wait for responses.) Yes,
it is the /h/ sound. (Provide sound if students did
now know.) You try (model again, as needed).
Alphabet Knowledge & Letter Formation
Instructional Notes
Students who can write letters independently from
memory will be able to recognize the letters more
readily.
Introduction: Lines
Today we will practice writing different kinds of
lines. This will help us write some letters of the
alphabet.
Vertical Lines
This is a vertical line (Show example). A vertical
line goes up and down. This is how we make a
vertical line (model while reciting directions): Start
at the top. Go straight down. Stop at the bottom.
Let's try skywriting a vertical line. Have students
stand up to sky write. Recite the formation
directions while making the motion. Let's try
tracing a vertical line with our finger on the table
(or another surface). Let's try some more.
Have students practice making more vertical lines
in a variety of sizes (big and small) and on a variety
of surfaces (the wall, a tabletop, the floor, their
palm, etc.)
Horizontal Lines
A horizontal line goes from left to right or right to
left. This is how we make a horizontal line (model
while reciting directions): Start at the top. Go
straight this way (left to right) and stop.
Continued from previous column...
Let's try skywriting. Have students stand up to sky
write. Recite the formation directions while making
the motion.
Let's try tracing with our finger on the table
(or another surface). Let's try some more. Have
students practice a variety of sizes and on a variety
of surfaces.
Slanted Lines
These are slanted lines (show example). Slanted
lines look like a slide. We can make slanted lines
a few different ways. Sometimes we start at the
top and slide down to the right like this (model \ ).
Sometimes we start at the bottom and slide back
up to the left (model \ ). Let's try skywriting. Have
students skywrite slanted lines like this \ going top
to bottom then bottom to top.
Slanted lines can go the other way too. We can
start at the bottom and slide up to the right like
this (model /) and we can slide back down the
same way (model /). Let's try skywriting. Have
students skywrite slanted lines like this / going
bottom to top then top to bottom.
Let's try some more. Provide an opportunity for
varied practice making both types of slanted lines:
/\.

## Lesson getting_ready_c — Getting Ready Lesson C
```
lesson_id: getting_ready_c
lesson_day_count: 2
skill_focus: "digraphs/complex consonants"

#### Raw script
```
Instructional Notes
Sounds are produced by sending air up from the
lungs through the mouth or nose. Consonant
sounds disrupt the airflow in some way. The
manner of articulation is the way in which the
airstream is disrupted. Stops are consonant sounds
that are produced by completely stopping airflow.
Nasal, or nose, sounds are produced when the
air is shut off from coming out of our mouth, and
instead comes out through the nasal cavity.
For this lesson, the teacher will need to have the
column headers for the consonant grid and the row
headers (stop, nose, fricative, affricate, glide, and
liquid) already displayed. The teacher will also need
the following sound cards ready to be placed on the
consonant grid during the lesson: /p/, /b/, /t/, /d/,
If, /of, /n/, /n, and ff.
Students should be seated in an area where
they can clearly see the consonant grid and the
teacher's mouth.
Introduction: Manner of Articulation
Yesterday, we talked about the places we make
sounds in our mouth. Today, we will be talking about
sounds and the way (or manner) we make sounds.
Stop Sounds
Let's start with stop sounds. We call them stop
sounds, because we say them very quickly and we
completely stop the airflow in our mouths. We also
can't hold them out without making them sound
funny. The stop sounds are /p/, /b/, /t/, /d/, /k/,
and /g/. We will practice these sounds next.
/p/ & /b/: When you make the /p/ sound, like
at the beginning of the word pig, you put your
lips together, and make your lips pop like this...
(model). Your voice is turned off when you say /p/.
Watch me /p/. You try (/p/). When you make the
/b/ sound, like at the beginning of the word bee,
you make it in the same way as /p/, except your
voice is turned on. Watch me /b/. You try (/b/).
When we make /p/ or /b/, our lips look like this
(show picture cards). We will put these lips right
here under "Lips Together" and next to "Stop"
because that's how we make those sounds. Place
/p/ and /b/ picture cards on the sound wall.
'Continued from.
s
/t/ & /A/: The sounds /t/ and /d/ are very similar.
Just like /p/ and /b/, both /t/ and /d/ are called
stop sounds because we have to say them quickly
(make stop hand gesture). When you make the
/t/ sound, like at the beginning of the word tiger,
you tap your tongue behind your top teeth, and
you push gir out like this... (model). Your voice is
turned off when you say /t/. Watch me /t/. You
try (/t/). When you make the /d/ sound, like at
the beginning of the word dog, you make it in the
same woy as /t/, except your voice is turned on.
Watch me /d/. You try (/d/). When we make /t/ or
/d/, our lips look like this (show picture cards). We
will put these lips right here under "Tongue Behind
Top Teeth" and next to "Stop" because that's how
we make those sounds. Place /t/ and /d/ picture
cards on the sound wall.
/t/ & /g/: Both /k/ and /g/ are called stop sounds
because we have to say them quickly (make stop
hand gesture). When you make the /k/ sound,
like at the beginning of the word kite, you tap the
back part of your tongue against the roof of your
mouth, and you push air out like this...(model).
Your voice is turned off when you say /k/. Watch
me /k/. You try (/k/). When you make the /g/
sound, like at the beginning of the word ghost, you
make it in the same way as /k/, except your voice
is turned on. Watch me /g/. You try (/k/).When
we make /k/ or /g/, our lips look like this (show
picture cards). We will put these lips right here
under "Back of Tongue Lifted" and next to "Stop"
because that's how we make those sounds. Place
/k/ and /g/ picture cards on the sound wall.
Nose Sounds
Next, some sounds are made when the air is shut off
from coming out of our mouth, and instead the air
comes out through your nose. The nose sounds are
/m/], {n{, and /n/. We will practice these sounds next.
/m/: The first nose sound we make with our lips
together is the /m/ sound. The /m/ sound is a sound
that we make by pushing air through our nose (make
nose sound gesture). When we say /m/, our voice is
on and we hold it out. It's a continuous sound.
Continued on next page...
Continued from previous page...
Watch me /mmm/. You try (/mmm/). When we say
/m/, like at the beginning of the word mouse, our
lips also look like this (show picture card). We will
put these lips right here under "Lips Together" and
next to "Nose" because that's how we make this
sound. Place /m/ picture card on the sound wall.
We're going to put these lips right here under "Lips
Together" and next to "Nose" because that's how we
make this sound. (Place /m/ mouth on sound wall.)
/n/: Next, we have /n/. The /n/ sound is very
similar to the /m/ sound. We make the /n/ sound
by pushing air through our nose, but our tongue is
behind our top teeth. Our voice is on. Watch me
/nnn/. You try (/nnn/). When we make /n/, like at

#### Raw script
```
Instructional Notes
The manner of articulation is the way in which
the airstream is disrupted. Fricatives are sounds
produced with friction created by forcing air
through a constricted space. Affricates combine the
features of the stop and the fricative, stopping air
and then releasing it through a constricted space.
For this lesson, the teacher will need to have the
column and row headers for the consonant grid
already displayed. The teacher will also need the
following sound cards ready to be placed on the
consonant grid during the lesson: /f/, /v/, /th/, /th/,
/s/, /2/, /sh/, /zby, [hf /ch/, and fi.
Students should be seated in an area where
they can clearly see the consonant grid and the
teacher's mouth.
Introduction
Yesterday, we talked about the way we can make
sounds that stop and others that we make through
our nose. Today, we will be talking about how to
make two more sounds: Fricatives and Affricates.
Let's start with fricative sounds. We call them
fricative sounds because when we say them we
push air through a small opening and it makes
friction (make fricative hand gesture). The fricative
sounds are /f/, /v/, /th/, /th/, /s/, /z/, /sh/, /zh/,
and /h/. We will practice these sounds next.
/£/ & /v/: When you make the /f/ sound, like at the
beginning of the word fish, you put your teeth on
your bottom lip, and you push air out like this...
(model). Your voice is turned off when you say /f/.
Watch me /f/. You try (/f/). When you make the /v/
sound, like at the beginning of the word volcano,
you make it in the same way as /f/, except your
voice is turned on. Watch me /v/. You try (/v/).
When we make /f/ or /v/, our lips look like this
(show picture cards). We will put these lips right
here under "Teeth on Lips" and next to "Fricative"
because that's how we make those sounds. Place
/f/ and /v/ picture cards on the sound wall.
/th/ & /th/:
Just like /f/ and /v/, both /th/ and
/th/ are called fricative sounds because, when we
say them, we push air through a small opening,
and it makes friction (make fricative hand gesture).
50

When you make the /th/ sound, like at the

make the /th/ sound, like at the beginning of the
words this and that, you make it in the same way
as /th/, except your voice is turned on (model).

sheep, you round your lips, you pull back your

picture cards).

Sound Wall, continued
Continued from previous page...
We will put these lips right here under "Back of
the Throat" and next to "Fricative" because that
is how we make this sound. Place /sh/ and /zh/
picture cards on the sound wall.
/h/: The last fricative sound is /h/. When you
make the /h/ sound, you open your lips like this and
push air out from the back of your throat like this...
(model and show picture card). Your voice is turned
off when you say /h/, like at the beginning of the

sounds. Place /h/ picture card on the sound wall.
Next, we have affricates. Affricates are like
fricatives combined with stops. Remember, for
fricative sounds, we push air through a small
opening, and it makes friction...
Continued from previous column...
(make fricative hand gesture), and we say stop
sounds quickly (make stop hand gesture). So, when
you say an affricate sound, you push air through a
small opening very quickly.
/eh/ & /j/: The sounds /ch/ and /j/ are affricates.
When you make the /ch/ sound, like at the
beginning of the word chicken, you round your lips,
you pull back your tongue, and you push air out
quickly like this...(model). Your voice is turned off
when you say /ch/. Watch me /ch/. You try (/ch/).
When you make the /j/ sound, like at the beginning
of the word jar, you make it in the same way as
/ch/, except your voice is turned on. Watch me /j/.
You try (/j/). When we make /ch/ or /j/, our lips look
like this (show picture cards). We will put these lips
right here under "Lips Rounded and Tongue Pulled
Back" and next to "Affricate" because that's how
we make those sounds. Place /ch/ and /j/ picture
cards on the sound wall.
Alphabet Knowledge & Letter Formation

5: New Concept
Introduction
Suffixes are added to the end of a word to change
the word's meaning. The suffix -ISH means
somewhat like or likely to. If you say someone is
acting childish it means they are behaving like a
child. If you describe something as greenish it means
the color is somewhat like green but not quite.
| do: pinkish
I do: greenish
We do: grayish, selfish,
We do: brownish, foolish
boyish, sluggish, newish

"Ys The suffix -Y means
Instructional Notes
The suffixes -Y and -LY both create describing
words. -Y is added to nouns to make them
adjectives.
-LY is added to adjectives to make
them adverbs.
When the suffix -LY is added to a word already
ending in -Y use the Y to
| spelling change rule:
noise (noun) > noisy (adjective) > noisily (adverb)
greed (noun) > greedy (adjective) > greedily (adverb)

- Step 53: --- CONTINUED BLOCK ---

#### Raw script
```
Instructional Notes
Continued from previous column...
The manner of articulation is the way in which
the airstream is disrupted. Glides and liquids are
produced when the tongue approaches a point of
articulation within the mouth but does not come
close enough to obstruct or constrict the flow of air
enough to create turbulence.
For this lesson, the teacher will need to have the
column and row headers for the consonant grid
already displayed. The teacher will also need the
following sound cards ready to be placed on the
consonant grid during the lesson: /y/, /wh/, /w/, /V/,
and /r/.
Students should be seated in an area where
they can clearly see the consonant grid and the
teacher's mouth.
Introduction
Yesterday, we talked about the way we can make
sounds fricative and affricate sounds. Today, we
will learn about the last two ways we can make
sounds: Glide and Liquid.
Another type of sound is a glide. Glide sounds glide
or move into the next sound.
/y/: The first glide sound is the /y/ sound, as in
yellow. When you make the /y/ sound, you round
your lips, you pull back your tongue like this...
(model and show picture card). Your voice is
turned on when you say /y/, like at the beginning
of the word yes, and the sound glides into the
next sound. Watch me /y/. You try (/y/). We will
put these lips right here under "Lips Rounded and
Tongue Pulled Back" and next to "Glide" because
that's how we make those sounds. Place /y/
picture card on the sound wall.
/w/: The next glide sound is the /w/ sound, as
in watermelon. When you make the /w/ sound,
touch the back part of your tongue against the
roof of your mouth, you round your lips like this...
(model and show picture card). Your voice is turned
on when you say /w/, and the sound glides into the
next sound. Watch me /w/. You try (/w/).
52
We're going to put these lips right here under
"Back of Tongue Lifted" and next to "Glide"
because that's how we make those sounds. Place
/w/ picture card on the sound wall.
/wth/; The last glide sound is the /wh/ sound, as
in whistle. When you make the /wh/ sound, touch
the back part of your tongue against the roof of
your mouth, you round your lips like this... (model
and show mouth picture). Your voice is turned on
when you say /wh/, and the sound glides into the
next sound. Watch me /wh/. You try (/wh/). We
will put these lips right here under "Back of Tongue
Lifted" and next to "Glide" because that's how we
make those sounds. Place /wh/ picture card on the
sound wall.
Liquid Sounds
We have one more way a sound can be made.
These are called liquid sounds. Liquid sounds are a
bit slippery like a liquid (make liquid hand gesture).
/¥/: /V is a liquid sound. When we make /I/, like
at the beginning of the word leaf, our lips look
like this (model and show picture card). When you
make the /I/ sound your voice is on. Watch me
/\/. You try (/I/). We're going to put these lips right
here under "Tongue Behind Top Teeth" and next to
"Liquid" because that's how we make this sound.
Place /I/ picture card on the sound wall.
/t/: [r{ is another liquid sound because it's a
little
bit slippery like a liquid (make liquid hand gesture).
When we make /r/, like at the beginning of the
word red, our lips look like this (model and show
picture card). When you make the /r/ sound, your
voice is on. Watch me /r/. You try (/r/). We're
going to put these lips right here under "Lips
Rounded and Tongue Pulled Back" and next to
"Liquid" because that's how we make this sound.
Place /r/ picture card on the sound wall.
Alphabet Knowledge & Letter Formation

Instructional Notes
This lesson is a review of the sound wail, the places
of articulation, the ways we articulate sounds (i.e.,
manner of articulation), and the consonant sounds
already introduced.
Teachers starting at Lesson 35 or later: While
reviewing the consonant sounds, add the
corresponding consonant grapheme under each
sound. (b,c, d, f,9,h,j,k,47,,p,755,t v, wy, z.)
Teachers starting at Lesson 1: These graphemes
are introduced in the alphabet unit (lessons 1-34)
and do not need to be added now.
Introduction: Review
Consonant sounds are made by moving the parts
of your mouth, like your teeth, lips, or tongue,
Continued from previous column...
usually so that one part of your mouth touches
another part. (Point to those headers on the
consonant grid.) When we move our mouth parts
to make consonant sounds, we also block air from
flowing out of our mouths. (Point to row headers
on the consonant grid.) Sometimes we block it all
the way, and sometimes we only block it part of
the way. For some consonant sounds, we'll turn
our voices on. (Point to the voiced sounds on the
consonant grid.) For others, we won't. (Point to
unvoiced on the consonant grid.) Let's review
the consonant sounds we talked about so far.
Have students practice producing sounds. Provide
corrective feedback about place and manner of
articulation as needed.

## Lesson getting_ready_g — Getting Ready Lesson G
```
lesson_id: getting_ready_g
lesson_day_count: 2
skill_focus: "digraphs/complex consonants"
materials: []
standards: []

#### Raw script
```
instructional Notes
Vowel sounds are made by vibrating our vocal
cords and fetting air flow freely out of our mouths.
There's no obstruction of air like you have with
consonants. All vowel sounds are voiced. Vowel
sounds are characterized by the location of
the tongue in the mouth, the tenseness of the
muscles, and the shape of the lips.
The teacher will be introducing the concept of a
vowel within this lesson. No materials are needed
for this part of the lesson.
Introduction: Vowels
Vowel sounds are made by vibrating our vocal
cords and letting air flow freely out of our mouths.
When we make a vowel sound, our lips might be
rounded like this /oo/, or wide open like this /6/, or
smiley like this /é/.
See if you can tell whether this is
a consonant
sound or a vowel sound. Think about whether
your tongue or lips or teeth touch, or if you're just
changing the shape of your lips. Ready?:
/fff/ (consonant), /44G/ (vowel),
/p/ (consonant),
/nnn/ (consonant), /666/ (vowel), /ch/ (consonant),
/d/ (consonant), /k/ (consonant), /66é/ (vowel).

## Lesson getting_ready_h — Getting Ready Lesson H
```
lesson_id: getting_ready_h
lesson_day_count: 2
skill_focus: "digraphs/complex consonants"
materials: []
standards: []
```

### Steps found (brief)
- Step 56: a
- Step 57: --- CONTINUED BLOCK ---

#### Raw script
```
instructional Notes
When our mouth muscles are tighter or more
tense, we make what we usually call long vowel
sounds. The tense or long vowel sounds are
essentially the same as their letter name. When
our mouth muscles are looser or more relaxed, we
make lax vowel sounds, or what we usually call
short vowel sounds. Although they are commonly
called "long" and "short," there's really not a
discernible difference in the length of the sounds.
Begin the Vowel Valley introduction with the mouth
pictures already in place and on display.
Introduction: The Vowel Valley
Vowel sounds are made by vibrating our vocal
cords and letting air flow freely out of our mouths.
The main differences between vowel sounds are
where our tongue is as we make the sound  --  how
high or low and whether it's in the front, middle, or
back of our mouth. This is why we arrange them in
the shape of a valley.
The sounds up here are made in the front, up high,
and with a smiley mouth. (Point to the /€/ mouth.)
This one is /é/. Watch me /é/. You try (/é/).
The ones down here are made with the tongue low
and the mouth open wide and round. (Point to the /6/
mouth.) This one is /6/. Watch me /6/. You try (/5/).
Continued from previous column...
The ones up here are made with the tongue in the
back with rounded lips. (Point to the /yG/.) This one
is /yG/. Watch me /yG/. You try (/yi/).
We're going to say all our vowel sounds. We'll start
with the vowel sounds where the tongue is high
and at the front of the mouth, move to the vowels
where the tongue is low in the middle, and finally
to the vowels where the tongue is high and at the
back. Ready? Point to each mouth picture as you
model the sound, and have students repeat the
sound: /é/, /i/, /a/, /é/, /4/, /i/, /6/, jaw], Jaf, /6/,
/oof, /G/, /ya/.
When our mouth muscles are tighter or more
tense, we make what we call long vowel sounds.
The long vowel sounds are the same as their letter
names (point to each mouth as you model the
sound, and have students repeat the sound): /@/,
/é/, fiJ, of, [ya].

relaxed, we make what we call short vowel sounds.
The short vowel sounds are (point to each mouth
as you model the sound, and have students repeat
the sound): /a/, /@/, /i/, /6/, /4/.
(Review all vowel sounds.)

## Lesson getting_ready_i — Getting Ready Lesson I
```
lesson_id: getting_ready_i
lesson_day_count: 2
skill_focus: "digraphs/complex consonants"
materials: []
standards: []
```

### Steps found (brief)
- Step 59: --- CONTINUED BLOCK ---

#### Raw script
```
Instructional Notes
In the very middle of the vowel valley, there is a
relaxed vowel sound called a schwa. The schwa
is an unstressed mid-central vowel sound (/a/). A
schwa is not the same as a short U sound (/ti/),
which is more distinct. The schwa is indistinct and,
most importantly, unstressed. The schwa sound
can be spelled with any vowel letter.
There are special kinds of vowel sounds:
diphthongs and r-controlled vowels. Diphthongs
are vowel sounds, in which two different sounds
glide together so quickly we can't separate them.
The sounds /oi/ and /ow/ are the best-known
diphthongs. R-controlled vowels function similarly
to diphthongs in that they are combinations of
sounds that can't be separated. We say that the
R "controls" the vowel. It's neither tense nor lax.
There are three main r-controlled vowel sounds:
Jer], far/, and /or/.
Have the Vowel Valley introduction with the mouth
pictures already in place and on display.
Introduction
Today we will learn about more vowel sounds.
Instructional Notes
Review the difference between straight and slanted
lines.
Introduction
Today we will practice making letters with slanted
lines.
Uppercase V
This is uppercase V (show V). What letter is this?
(uppercase V). Uppercase V
is two slanted lines
that touch at the bottom. We make uppercase V
like this (model while reciting directions): Start at
the top. Slant line down to the bottom. Slant line
up to the top (to the right). Have students practice
uppercase V with skywriting, finger tracing, and
pencils or markers. Students should recite the
directions as they make the letter.
Alphabet Knowledge & Letter Formation
In the very middle of the Vowel Valley we have a
super relaxed vowel sound called a schwa. (Point
to the schwa mouth.) We pronounce the schwa like
this: /a/. You try it (model again, as needed).
Special Cases
We also have two special kinds of vowel sounds:
diphthongs and r-controlled vowels. (Point to these
two sets of mouth pictures.)
Diphthongs: We'll keep the diphthongs over here.
(Point to the diphthong mouths.) These are sounds
that are too tricky to put in the valley because
of how they change from one mouth position to
another. We have two diphthongs here: /ow/ and
/oi/. You try it (model again, as needed).
R-Controlled Vowels: We'll keep r-controlled
sounds over here. (Point to the r-controlled
mouths.) These are sounds that are too tricky to
put in the valley because of how they combine
a vowel sound with a /r/ sound. We have three
r-controlled vowel sounds here: /ar/, /er/ and /or/.
You try it (model again, as needed).
Review all vowel sounds.
'Continued from previous column...
(Show uppercase V.) This is uppercase V. What
letter is this? (uppercase V.)
Uppercase W
This is uppercase W (show W). What letter is this?
(uppercase W). Uppercase W
is four slanted lines.
We make uppercase W
like this (model while
reciting directions): Start at the top. Slant line
down to the bottom. Slant line up to the top (to
the right). Slant line down to the bottom again.
Slant line up to the top (to the right). Have students
practice uppercase W with skywriting, finger
tracing, and pencils or markers. Students should
recite the directions as they make the letter. (Show
uppercase W.) This is uppercase W. What letter is
this? (uppercase W.)
Continued on next page...
Alphabet Knowledge & Letter Formation, continued
Uppercase X
This is uppercase X (show X). What letter is this?
(uppercase X). Uppercase X
is two slanted lines
that cross in the middle. We make uppercase X
like
this (mode! while reciting directions): Start at the
top. Slant line down to the bottom (left to right).
Hop back to the top. Slant line down the other way
(right to left). Have students practice uppercase
X with skywriting, finger tracing, and pencils or
markers. Students should recite the directions as

This is the letter M. M is a consonant. M spells /m/

Introduc tion
There are five vowels: A, E, 1,0, U. Each vowel has
along sound and a short sound. The long sound is
the vowel's name. Today we will practice reading
and spelling words with all five short vowel sounds.

of the word apple. When A comes before the
consonants M and N, the sound changes, like in the
words am and can. The short vowel
| spells /i/ like
in the beginning of the word itch. The short vowel O
spells /6/ like in the beginning of the word octopus.

Instructional Notes
The U in U_E words may be pronounced in two
different ways: /U/ as in 'tube' or /yG/ as in 'cube:
The /yu/ sound of U
in 'cube' is a diphthong, gliding
from one part of the mouth to another. The /G/
sound in 'tube' is not a diphthong.

5: New Concept
Introduction
We have learned that U spells /ti/ as in up. /G/ is
a short vowel sound. U also has two long vowel
sounds. Long U can be /ti/ as in spoon. Long U can
also be /yi/ as in unicorn.
U spells its long sounds when there is a silent E at
the end of a word, like in the words June and cube.
The silent
E makes vowels say their names, or long
vowel sounds.

5: New Concept
Introduction
We have learned many ways to spell the long A
/a/ sound: A_E like in cake, Al like in rain, AY like
in gray, and A in an open syllable, like the A in
acorn. Today we are going to learn five more, less
common, ways to spell the long A /G/ sound.
ei /@/: El can spell /G/ like in vein. El) only comes in
the middle of a word, like in rein and veil.
ey /a/: EY can spell /G/ like in hey. EY only comes
at the end of a word, like in they and obey.

5: New Concept
Introduction |
we know two sounds for long U: jal as flew and
/yi/ as in cube. Both of these sounds can be
spelled in multiple ways.
/a/: We already know four ways to spell the /ii/
sound: U_E as in rule, EW as in chew, UI as in fruit,
and UE as in blue.
OU is another way to spell /U/. We know OU spells
/ow/ as in shout. Sometimes, OU can also spell /G/
as in soup and group.
/yia/: We already know one way to spell the /ya/
sound: U_E as in cute.

Instructional Notes
The T in -TURE is commonly pronounced /ch/. This

5: New Concept
Introduction
Syllables are words or word parts with one vowel
sound. Learning about syliables helps us read and
spell.
Final stable syllables always come at the end of
a word. We know three final stable syllables, -LE,
-SION, and -TION. Today we will learn another final
stable syllable, -TURE.

#### Raw script
```
Not implemented until
4: Blending Drill
Initia 1
Final
Not implemented until Lesson 5 (VC/CVC)
5: New Concept
iction
This is the letter A. Ais a vowel. You know the
name of this letter already, "a." This is the long
vowel sound. Today we are going to read words
with the short vowel sound. The short vowel A
spells /G/
like in the beginning of the word apple.
The letter A can come in the middle of a word,
such as the words cat and map. The letter A can
also come at the beginning of a word, such as the
words at and as.
A Articulatory ¢
ens

This is the letter T. T is a consonant. T spells /t/ like
in the beginning of the word tiger. The letter T can
come at the end of a word, such as the words at

of a word, such as the words tap and top.

behind your top, front teeth like this...(model).

of your tray. Our new letter is S. Find the letter S
on your tray, pick it up, and put it at the bottom
of your tray. Repeat as needed, having students
practice finding letters and putting them back.

We have been learning new letters and sounds!
Today we will practice reading and spelling words
using the letters and sounds we know. When we
read words, we look at every letter, say each
sound, then blend the sounds together to read the
word. When we spell words, first we say the whole
word, next we say each sound we hear in the word,
and then we write a letter for each sound.

#### Raw script

| Nasalized A
Nasalized As: When M or N come after A, the A makes a whiny sound
Instructional Notes
This lesson focuses on the nasalized version of
short /@/. When the letter N or M follows A, the /G/
becomes nasalized and makes a slightly different
sound than the typical short /G/ (e.g., can vs. cat).
Students can now decode the word 'an. The words
'a' and 'an' are indefinite articles. The word 'a' is
used before a word beginning with a consonant
sound while 'an' is used before a word beginning
with a vowel sound (e.g., a bat, an apple).
instruction about when to use 'a' and 'an' should
be introduced separately when appropriate.
1: Phonemic Awareness
Biend SS

texts, like in the sentence "I am Sam."

=§ /s/: The grapheme S can spell /s/ at the end of words
Instructional Notes
5: New Concept
This lesson is students' first introduction to words
with four sounds.
This lesson focuses on S at the end of a word
representing the familiar /s/ sound. When S
is
added to the end of a word following a voiced
sound it represents the /z/ sound as in 'dogs' This
concept is addressed in Lesson 21.
Adding the suffix -S to a noun changes the noun
from singular to plural (e.g., cat, cats). Adding the
suffix -S to a verb changes the tense of the verb
(e.g., run, runs).
The suffix -ES is added to words ending in S, X, Z,
CH, and SH (Lesson 63).
The concepts of plural and tense should be
introduced separately when appropriate.
1: Phonemic Awareness
Blend

S is the most common spelling of the /z/ sound.
S only represents /z/ at the end of a word when it
follows a voiced sound.
The sounds /s/ and /z/ are produced the same way,
except /s/ is unvoiced and /z/ is voiced.

| k /k/
k /kk/: The grapheme K
spells /k/
Instructional Notes
K spells /k/ when followed by E, t, or Y and C spells
/k/ when followed by A, O, or U (e.g., kit, cat).
The -CK pattern spells /k/ at the end of a word
immediately after a short vowel sound (Lesson 44).
This lesson is students' first introduction to
words with consonant clusters. It is important for
students to recognize the individual sounds in
consonant clusters. It is not necessary for students
to memorize which consonant pairs are commonly
blended together.
1: Phonemic Awareness
Blend

own sounds, /s/ /k/, like in the beginning of skip

can't be stretched out (use stop hand motion).

standards: []

### Steps found (brief)
- Step 1: Phonemic Awareness
- Step 2: Visual Drill
- Step 3: Auditory Drill
- Step 4: Blending Drill
- Step 5: New Concept
- Step 6: Word Work
- Step 7: Irregular Words
- Step 8: Connected Text

#### Raw script
```
| qu /kw/
qu /kw/: The grapheme QO
followed by U
spells /kw/
Instructional Notes
in English, the letter Q is consistently paired with
the letter U. The letter combination QU represents
the combination of two sounds /k/ and /w/. Like x,
this is treated differently in the lesson, and you will
see two different notations: /kw/ and /k/ /w/. This is
intentional.
Blending skills are used for decoding. When
students are decoding 'quit,' and they see the
letters QU, they need to say /kw/ and blend this
combination with the other sounds. So, for the
phoneme blending and decoding activities,
pronounce the /kw/ together.
Segmenting skills are used for encoding. When
students are segmenting the phonemes in a
spoken word with /k/ and /w/, they must recognize
that these two sounds together are almost
always represented with QU. So, for the phoneme
segmenting and spelling tasks, pronounce /k/ and
/w/ separately.
1: Phonemic Awareness
Biend
Segment
[kw] [i/ [t/ (quit)
queen (/k/ /w/ /é/ /n/)

5: New Concept
This is the letter Q.
Q is a consonant. In English
words, Q is followed by a U. QU spells /kw/ like in
the beginning of the word queen. QU comes at the
beginning of a word, such as the words quiz and
quilt.
Q and U almost always go together, so when we
make the sounds, they sound like a /k/ and a /w/
(be sure to model the /w/ as a tight /o00/). So,
you'll round your lips tight and raise your tongue
up in the back like this...(model /kw/ -- be sure to
model as a very tight /kooo/, not as /kwuh/). Be
sure your voice is on when you make the /kw/
sound. Watch me /kw/. You try /kw/.
Sou
Because the grapheme Q represents the
combination of two sounds: /k/ and /w/, it is not
included on the sound wall.
Review Q: Start at the top. Curve this way
(counterclockwise). Go all the way around. Stop at
the top. Jump to the bottom (in the middle). Slant
a line down this way (left to right).
Introduce q: Begin at the middle line. Start at the
top. Curve all the way around. Straight line down
below the line. Curve up this way (left to right).

Articulatory Gesture

this.../fff/. Have students use mirrors to help them

| FLSZ Spelling Rule
FLSZ Spelling Rule: F, L, S, and Z are doubled after a short vowel sound
Instructional Notes
This lesson is students' first introduction to the
concept that two letters can be combined to
represent one sound.
It is common for other consonants to be doubled,
especially as part of a closed syllables in
a word
with a suffix (e.g., rubber, stopped), but the focus
of this lesson is the set of consonants that are
consistently doubled at the end of a short word.

5: New Concept
Introd duct ion ae.
Let's look at some letters we
2 already know.
F spells /f/, L spells /|/,.S spells /s/ and Z spells
/z/. Something tricky happens when these sounds
come after a short vowel sound.
Continued from previous column.
When we hear /f/, /I/, /s/, or /z/ at the end of a
word after a short vowel sound, we use double
letters, or the same letter two times, to spell that
sound. A helpful way to remember these letters that
are doubled at the end of words is with the sentence
"Jeff will pass Buzz.' Discuss how the letters FF, LL,
SS, and ZZ come after short vowel sounds.
When you see these double letters FF, LL, SS, and
2Z, you sound them out the same way as F,L,S,Z.
When you hear /f/, /I/, /s/, or /z/ at the end of a
word after a short vowel, you use double letters to
spell it.

Ss
We have learned that we use LL to spell /I/ after a
short vowel sound, like in the words fell and will.
Sometimes, when LL comes after the vowels A, O,
and U, the LL changes the vowel sound.
-all: A-L-L spells /all/ like in the words call and
small. Say -all (-all).
~oli: O-L-L can have the short /6/ sound like in doll.
But the O in O-L-L is usually pronounced /6/ like in
the words roll and troll. Say -dil (-dll).
"ull: U-L-L often has the short /U/ sound like in dull
or skull. But the U in U-L-L is sometimes pronounced
/00/ like in the words pull and full. Say -ull (-ull).

troduction
roe a consonant digraph is two ars
that come together to make one sound. You have
already learned about the consonant digraphs CK
/k/ like duck, SH /sh/ like sheep, and TH /th/ like

Gesture
To make the /ch/ sound, put your teeth together
and round your lips just like the /sh/ sound...
(model). Pull your tongue back toward the back of
your mouth, but the tip touches behind your teeth.
Your tongue will pull quickly away from your
teeth as you release the air. Be sure your voice is
off. The /ch/ sound is a stop sound that can't be
stretched out (use stop hand motion). Watch me
/ch/. You try /ch/.

me /www/. You try /www/. Be sure to model the
/w/ as a very tight fooo/, not as /wuh/.

| ng /n/
ng /y/: The grapheme NG spells /n/
Instructional Notes
The /n/ can sometimes distort the sound of the
preceding vowel. The extent of the distortion varies
by region, but ANG is often pronounced with a long
/G/ sound (e.g., sang), and ING is often pronounced
with a long /é/ sound (e.g., thing). It is important to
be aware of your own pronunciation as you model
the sound for students.
Although there is no /g/ pronounced in most words
with NG (e.g., hanger), some words with NG are
pronounced with /n/+/g/ (e.g., anger, hungry).

/G/ sound (e.g., sank), and INK is often pronounced
with a long /6/ sound (e.g., think). It is important to
be aware of your own pronunciation as you model
the sound for students.

5: New Concept
introduction: Digraphs
Today we will review reading words with CK, SH,
TH, CH, WH, PH, NG, and NK.
ck /Ik/: When C and K are together at the end of
a word, they spell /k/ like at the end of the word
duck. CK can only come at the end of a word.
sh /sh/: The consonant digraph SH spells the /sh/
sound, like at the beginning of the word sheep. SH
can come at the beginning or end of a word.
th /th/, /th/: The consonant digraph TH spells two
sounds.
Continued from previous column...
The unvoiced TH spells /th/ like at the beginning of
the word thumb. The unvoiced /th/ can come at the
beginning or end of a word. The voiced TH spells
/th/ like at the beginning of the word this. Voiced
/th/ can only come at the beginning of a word.
ch /ch/: The consonant digraph CH spells /ch/ like
at the beginning of the word chicken. CH can come
at the beginning or end of a word.
wh /w/: The consonant digraph WH spells /w/ like
at the beginning of the word whistle. WH can only
come at the beginning of a word.
ph /f/: The consonant digraph PH spells /f/ like at
the beginning of the word phone. PH can come at
the beginning or end of a word.
ng /n/: The sound /n/ is usually represented by
the letters NG, like at the end of the word ring. The
letters NG for the sound /n/ only come at the end
of a word.
nk /n/: The sound /n/ can also be represented by
the letter N, when it is combined with a K, like at
the end of the word think. The letters NK for the
sound /nk/ only come at the end of a word.
Introduction: Digraphs +r
Sometimes, the digraphs SH and TH are followed
by R at the beginning of a word. These sounds can
be tricky to blend together.
SH is followed by R at the beginning of words like
shrimp and shrub. Say shrimp (shrimp). Say shrub
(shrub).
TH is followed by R at the beginning of words like
three and thrill. Say three (three). Say thrill (thrill).
Articulatory Gesture
Refer to sound wall while reviewing concepts.
Refer to the picture card for each digraph on the
consonant grid. Add grapheme cards if not already
displayed.
Letter Formation
Practice letter formation, as needed.

| a_e /G/
a
e /@/: Reading and spelling words with the A_E pattern
Instructional Notes
This is students' first introduction to words with
long vowel sounds.
The long /G/ sound is technically a diphthong,
meaning the vowel sound glides from one part of
the mouth to another. As you model how to hold it
out as a continuous sound, your mouth will change
positions.
1: Phonemic Awareness

S: New Concept
Introduction
We have learned that A spells /G/ as in apple. /@/ is
a short vowel sound. A also has a long vowel sound:
/G/ as in acorn. The long vowel sound is the same as
the vowel's name A, /G/.
Aspells /4/ when there is a silent E at the end of a
word, like in the words made and tape. The silent E
makes vowels say their names, or long vowel sounds.
You can use the terms "magic E" or "bossy E" to
describe the silent E.
It is important to notice when there is an E at the
end of a word because it will tell us to say the long
vowel sound like in the words cake, same, and
made. Remember, the E does not make a sound, it
is silent.
Display the word 'tap'. This word is tap /t/ /@/ /p/
tap. The A is making a short sound: /4/. Watch
what happens when
| add silent E. Display the
word 'tape'. Tap becomes tape /t/ /a/ /p/ tape.
The silent E made the A say its name. Repeat this
modeling with cap > cape; mad > made.
To make the long /G@/ sound, open your mouth just
a little, with your lips pulled back and tense. The tip
of your tongue starts at the bottom of your mouth
behind your teeth, and as your mouth narrows, the
tongue moves up like this... (model). Be sure your
voice is on. Like all vowels, /G/ is a continuous
sound that can be stretched out (use continuous
hand motion). Watch me /G/. You try /G/.

the // picture card.

#### Raw script

| u_e /G/, /vu/
Continued from previous column...
You can use the terms "magic E" or "bossy E" to
describe the silent E.
We hear the /0/ sound in words like flute and tube.
We hear the /yU/ sound in words like use and
mute. When we see words with the U_E pattern,
we can be flexible and try both sounds for long U
to see which one sounds right.
As you read and spell words, have students identify
which sound for U they hear. Pronunciations may
vary depending on dialect.
Articulatory Gesture
1 /ia/: To make the long /&/ sound, round your lips
tightly and pull the back of your tongue toward the
roof of your mouth, with the sides of your tongue
touching the insides of your teeth like this...
(model). Be sure your voice is on. Like ali vowels,
/a/ is
a continuous sound that can be stretched out
(use continuous hand motion). Watch me /ti/. You
try /G/.
u /yii/: To make the long /yt/ sound, begin with
a tight smile with your tongue pulled back toward
the roof of your mouth, as if you're going to say
/@/, then round your lips tightly and move into /G/
like this...(model). Be sure your voice is on. Like
all vowels, /yd/ is a continuous sound that can be
stretched out (use continuous hand motion). Watch
me /yG/. You try /yi/.

card.
Continued from previous column...
The -ES ending has two sounds. The E makes a
schwa sound and the S makes the /z/ sound. A
schwa is a common sound in words with more
than one syllable.
The schwa is the most relaxed of all sounds. The
schwa is made right in the middle of your mouth,
with no stretching or tightening of your lips or
tongue. To make the schwa /a/ sound, open
your mouth just a
little bit, like this...(model). Be
sure your voice is on. Unlike all the other vowel
sounds, we don't really stretch out the schwa. We
say it more quickly. Watch me /a/. You try /a/.
Sometimes, a schwa can sound a
little like a short
u /0/ or a short i /i/, but it's actually more relaxed
than either of those. Try again /a/.
When we add -ES at the end of a word, the E
is
pronounced with a schwa. Listen to how
I say the
second syllable of these words: glasses, buzzes,
fetches. Did you hear the /a/?

5: New Concept
Introduction
Continued from previous column...
Say each word aloud and count the syllables with
your students. See lesson slide deck for visual aids.
Robot = 2, Octopus = 3, Macaroni = 4, Shoe = 1,
Umbrella = 3, Watermelon = 4, Zebra = 2,
Helicopter = 4
Closed Syllables: A closed syllable has only one
vowel and the vowel is followed by one or more
consonants. in a closed syllable, the vowel sound
is short. Remember, the short vowel sounds are
/4/, /&/, [i/, /5/, /4/. Hop, kick, and fast are all
examples of closed syllables. They have one vowel
followed by at least one consonant and the vowel
makes a short sound.
Open Syllables: An open syllable has only one
vowel and the vowel is the last letter in the
syllable. In an open syllable, the vowel sound is
long. Remember, the long vowel sounds are /a/,
/@/, [i/, /5/, Jaf. Hi, go, and she are examples of
open syllables. They have one vowel at the end
and the vowel makes a long sound.
As you read and spell, reinforce why the vowel
sound is long or short by recognizing whether the
syllable is open or closed.

Syllables: A syllable is
a word or a part of a word
with one vowel sound. A syllable can be pushed out
in one breath. Your chin will usually drop slightly as
you say each syllable in a word. Let's practice.

We have been learning about closed and open

'Continued from f previous column...
Met is a closed syllable. An open syllable has one
vowel that makes a long sound and ends with that
vowel. Me is an open syilable.
We can combine open and closed syllables to
make longer words.
1.

is an open syllable.

you get the word beyond. What is the word?
(beyond). The word beyond has an open
syllable, be, and a closed syllable, yond.
We have also learned that in many two-syllable
words, one syllable is stressed or emphasized more
than the other. This means that we say it a little
bit louder and hold it out just a little bit longer, like
-bout in about. The other syllable is pronounced
as a schwa. The A doesn't represent the /a/ or /a/
sounds. It represents the /a/. Listen: about.
The schwa can be spelled by any vowel letter: A, E,
1, O, U, and even Y. Watch me read these words. Can
you tell which letter is making a schwa sound? open
(E), bonus (U), human (A), demand (E) denim (|).
As you read and spell, guide students in breaking
apart words into syllables and listening for the
schwa sound

Today we will review new ways to spell familiar sounds.
We have learned that the consonant digraph
CH spells /ch/ like at the beginning of the word
chicken. CH can come at the beginning of a word,
such as chip and chin. CH can also come at the end
of a word, as in the words such and rich.
The grapheme TCH is another way to spell /ch/.
TCH is a trigraph, which means it is three letters
that make one sound. The trigraph TCH only comes
at the end of a word immediately after a short
vowel sound, as in the words catch and pitch.

can't be stretched out (use stop hand motion). Be
sure your voice is off. Watch me /ch/. You try /ch/.

displayed.

/i/ \ike in the word jar. The consonant G also spells

word cage.
The grapheme DGE is another way to spell /j/. DGE
is a trigraph, which means it is three letters that
make one sound. The trigraph DGE only comes at
the end of a word immediately after a short vowel
sound, as in the words dodge and bridge.

-le: Reading and spelling words with the LE ending
Instructional Notes
Many multisyllabic words ending in -LE include
double consonants. This typically happens when
the first syllable is closed and has the short vowel
sound (e.g., little, puzzle). This does not happen
when the closed syllable ends in a consonant
cluster or digraph (e.g., sample, bundle).
1: Phonemic Awareness

5: New Concept
introduction & Articulatory Gesture
Remember, a syliable is a word or part of a word
with one vowel sound. We have been learning
about different types of syllables, which helps us
read and spell big words.
Continued from p previous column...
Remember, closed syllables end in one or more
consonants and the vowel sound is short. Open
syllables end in a vowel and the vowel sound is long.
Another type of syllable is called a final stable
syllable. The name helps us remember what it is:
Final means last, so it's the last syllable in a word.
Stable means stays the same, so the sound is
always the same. One common final stable syllable
is consonant + LE. The LE spelis the /I/ sound like
at the end of apple and puzzle.
Remember, the /I/ sound is a continuous sound
that can be stretched out. Watch me /I/. You try /I/.
To decode words with the consonant + LE pattern,
we can cover up the last three letters (the final
consonant + LE) and look at the first syllable. We
can figure out if the first syllable is open or closed
so we know what sound the vowel will make. Then
we read each syllable and blend it together.
Watch me: (display the word 'table') I'm going to
cover up the last three letters, -BLE, so now
| have
TA-. That's an open syllable so
| say /ta/. Now|
read the next syllable: -BLE spells /bl/. Now
| blend
it together: table.

going to cover up the last three letters: -DLE. Now |
have MID-. That's a closed syllable so
| say
/mid/. The next syllable is -DLE /dl/. Now
| blend it
together: middle.

When we put the /r/ sound after a vowel, it
completely changes the way the vowel sounds.
It
makes what we call an "r-controlled" vowel.
ar /ar/: The r-controlled vowel AR usually
represents the /ar/ sound. AR can come in the
middle of a word, such as the words bark and part.
AR can sometimes come at the end of a word, such
as the words far and star. AR can sometimes come
at the beginning of a word, such as the words art
and arm.
or, ore /or/: Another r-controlled vowel sound is
/or/. OR usually represents the /or/ sound. OR can
come in the middle of a word, such as the words
fork and corn. OR can sometimes come at the be-
ginning of a word, such as the word orb.
OR can also come at the end of a word, such as the
word for. However, when we hear /or/ at the end
of a word it is usually spelled with an E, such as the
words more and store.
Articulatory Gesture: ar
To make the /ar/ sound, we start with our jaw
dropped and our mouth open. Then, we raise the
sides of our tongue and partially close our mouth
to make the /r/ part of the sound. Your voice is on.
Watch me /ar/. You try /ar/.

rounded, almost like we're going to say /aw/, but
the /r/ pulis the sides of our tongue up to say /or/.

We are learning that R can change how a vowel
sounds. We learned AR spells /ar/. When that
happens it is called an "r-controlled" vowel
because the R takes over the vowel's sound.
Another r-controlled vowel sound is /or/.
OR usually represents the /or/ sound.
OR can come in the middle of a word, such as the
words fork and corn. OR can sometimes come at
the beginning of a word, such as the word orb.
OR can also come at the end of a word, such as the
word for. However, when we hear /or/ at the end
of a word it is usually speiled with an E, such as the
words more and store.
For the /or/ sound, we begin with our lips rounded,
almost like we're going to say /aw/, but the /r/ pulls
the sides of our tongue up to say /or/. Your voice
is on. Listen: /or/. The /or/ sound is a continuous
sound that can be stretched out (use continuous
hand motion). Watch me /or/. You try /or/.
Add 'or' and 'ore' under /or/.

When spelling words with the /er/ sound, encourage
students to try multiple spellings (e.g., ER, IR, UR)
to find one that looks right and then confirm the
spelling using a dictionary or spellcheck.

often spelled SE. This pattern will occur in words
with r-controlled vowels (e.g., Curve, nurse).

There are two other graphemes that can also spell

ir /er/: (R can also be used to spell the /er/ sound.

raised up for the /r/ part of the sound. Your voice

5: New Concept
Introduction
We have been learning that when we put the /r/
sound after o vowel, it completely changes the
way the vowel sounds. It makes what we call an
"r-controlled" vowel.
er: The /er/ sound is an r-controlled vowel sound.
ER is the most common spelling of the /er/ sound.
ER can come in the middle of a word, such as the
words verb and fern. ER can also come at the end
of a word, such as the words her and per.
r
Articulatory
a
| Sour
Add 'or' under Jer/ (with 'er', 'ir', and 'ur')
I
S
Practice letter formation, as needed.
| Read
ir: There are two other graphemes that can also
spell the /er/ sound. IR can also be used to spell
the /er/ sound. IR can come in the middle of a
word, such as the words girl and bird. IR can also
come at the end of a word, such as the words sir
and stir.
ur: UR can also be used to spell the /er/ sound.
UR can come in the middle of a word, such as
the words turn and hurt. UR can come at the
beginning of a word, such as the words urn and
urge. UR can also come at the end of a word, such
as the words fur and blur.
or: We know the grapheme OR. Usually, OR spells
/or/. Sometimes, OR can spell the /er/ sound just
like ER, IR, and UR spell /er/. OR spells /er/ in
words that start with W. The OR /er/ sound can
come in the middle of a word, such as the words
work and word. When we see OR after W we say
/er/. When we hear /er/ after
W we spell it OR.
For the /er/ sound, we're combining an /ii/ sound
with an /r/ sound to say /er/. We begin with our
lips pulled forward for the /U/ part, but then our
tongue pulls back and the sides of the tongue are
raised up for the /r/ part of the sound. Your voice
is on. Listen: /er/. The /er/ sound is a continuous
sound that can be stretched out (use continuous
hand motion). Watch me /er/. You try /er/.

We have also learned that Y spells /é/ at the end
of two-syllable words like at the end of puppy.
Vowel teams are two or more letters that work
together to make one vowel sound.
ee /8/: The vowel team EE spells /é/. EE usually
comes in the middle of a word, like in need and
feet. EE can also come at the end of a word,
like in see and tree. EE sometimes comes at the
beginning of a word, like in eel.
en /8/: The vowel team EA also spells /@/. EA
usually comes in the middle of a word, like in mean
and team. EA sometimes comes at the beginning
of a word like in eat and each. EA sometimes
comes at the end of a word like in tea and flea.
ey /8/: The vowel team EY also spells /€/. EY only

money and turkey.

or more tense, we make long vowel sounds. Like

aw /aw/: The sound /aw/ can be spelled with the

| introc jluction
'Today we are going to learn a new sound /oi/. Joif
is a diphthong. This is a kind of vowel sound that
glides, which means our mouths move a
little bit as
we make the sound.
oi /oi/: The letters Ol together spell the /oi/ sound.
Ol usually comes in the middle of a word, such as

kn /n/: The consonant N spells /n/ like in the word

sound, like in the word knee. In this grapheme, the
K is silent. The grapheme KN for the /n/ sound can
only come at the beginning of a word, such as the
words knob and knit.

## Lesson 099 — -s/-es
```
lesson_id: 099
lesson_day_count: 2
skill_focus: "affixes & morphology"
materials: []
standards: []
```

### Steps found (brief)
- Step 0: a (/6/), oe (/5/)
- Step 1: Phonological Awareness
- Step 2: Visual Drill
- Step 3: Auditory Drill
- Step 4: Blending Drill
- Step 5: New Concept
- Step 6: Word Work
- Step 8: EE
- Step 27: 9

#### Raw script
```
-s/-eS: The suffixes -S and -ES make nouns plural and change a verb's tense
Instructional Notes
The purpose of this lesson is for students to
understand the morphemes -S and -ES as
suffixes that change tense or make words plural.
The reading and spelling of words with -ES was
addressed in Lesson 63.
The suffix -ES is added to words that end in CH, SH,
S, X, or Z. When the suffix -S is added to words that
end in silent E, the sound of the E may change (e.g.,
house > houses; place > places).
At this point, students can also decode the
morphemes -ING and -ED. These are both suffixes
added to verbs to change their tense (jump,
jumping, jumped).
Grammar concepts such as parts of speech, verb
tense, and proper use of helping verbs should be
addressed separately when appropriate.
1: Phonological Awareness
Segment
cooks (/k/ /oo/ /k/ /s/)
tells (/t/ /8/ /I/ /z/)
couches (couch-es)
couch (/k/ /ow/ /ch/)
es (/a/ /z)
3: Auditory Drill
/w] Jer] [af /z/ (words)
/s{ /8/ [nf [d/ [2] (sends)
Ir] [af {sh{ (rush)
Jaf /2] (es)
rush-es (rushes)
2: Visual Drill
Phonemes
{nf (n, kn), ff (r, we),
{m/f (m, mb), /oi/ (i, oy),
/ow/ (ou, ow), /&/ (e, ea),
/@/ (e, y, ee, ea, ey),
/aw/ (au, aw, augh),
/Q] (u, 00, ew, ui, Ue),
[8/ (0,9)
Graphemes
kn (/n/), wr (/r/), mb (/m/),
ou (/ow/), ow (/5/, /ow/),
oi (/oi/), oy (/oi/), a (/a/,
/aj, [9/), ea (/8/, /8/),
au (/aw/), aw (/aw/),
augh (/aw/), ew (/G/),
ui (/G/), ue (//), 00 (/oo/,
/O/),u (/a/, /O/, yay,
[o0/), ie (/i/), igh (/1/),
0a (/6/), oe (/5/)
4: Blending Drill
Use word chains from previous lessons to review
blending with previously taught concepts. Provide
ample practice with a variety of vowel patterns.
5: New Concept
Introduction
Morphemes are word parts that change a word's
meaning. A suffix is
a morpheme we add to the
end of a word.
We already know a few suffixes: -S and -ES. When
we add -S/-ES to nouns, the noun changes from
singular to plural (e.g., cat, cats). Remember,
nouns are words we use to identify people,
animals, places, and things. Dog, shirt, and box
are all nouns.
The suffixes -S/-ES can also be added to verbs.
Verbs are words we use to describe an action
or something that is happening. Run, sing, and
bite are verbs. They describe actions. We change
the endings of verbs depending on whether we
are describing our own actions or other people's
actions and whether we are describing actions in
the past, present, or future. This is called changing
the verb's tense.
Usually, simple present tense verbs do not have an
ending:
| run, you run, we run, they run. When we
use @ person's name or the pronouns he, she, or it,
we change the verb ending to -S or -ES: He runs.
She runs. It runs.
The rule for adding -ES is the same for verbs as it
is for nouns. We add -ES to verbs that end in CH,
SH, S, X, or Z: wishes, fixes, passes.
\ do: girls, fixes
We do: parts, days,
sounds, looks, mixes,
rushes
I do: classes, grows
We do: parts, dresses,
brushes, spends, shows,
screams, matches,
passes, rushes
6: Word Work
Word Sort
Read and sort the following words with -S and -ES
endings into nouns and verbs. See lesson slide deck
for graphic organizer.
rushes, dogs, lamps, mixes, glasses, fixes, cooks,
jumps, couches
Continued on next page...

-er/-est: The suffixes -ER and -EST make adjectives comparative

-less: The suffix -LESS is pronounced /las/ with the

Continued from previous column...

sleep. If you are anxious or excited you may have a
sleepless night because you cannot fall asleep.

U making a schwa sound, like in the word helpful.
~FUL means full of. Helpful means full of help. Ifa
person is being helpful they are giving a lot of help
to you.

4: Blending Drill
Use word chains from previous lessons to review

5: New Concept
Adjectives are words that give us more
information about people, places, and things.
7: Irregular Words
yf
1}
iOUS
fumyr
Adjectives describe nouns (people, places, and
things). For example, in the sentences "I see a red
car.
| see a blue car," the adjectives red and blue
are used to describe the noun car. They give us
more information. What kind of car? A red car. A
blue car.
Morphemes are word parts that change a word's
meaning. A suffix is
a morpheme we add to
the end of a word. We can add the suffixes -ER
and -EST to adjectives when we are making
comparisons.
-er: The suffix -ER is pronounced /er/ like in the
word taller. -ER means more than. For example,
in the sentence "Mae is taller than Jaime," taller
describes Mae's height by comparing it to Jamie's.
~est: The suffix -EST is pronounced /ast/ with
the E making a schwa sound, like in the word
fastest. -EST means the most. For example, in the
sentence "Ty is the fastest person on the team,"
fastest is an adjective used to describe Ty by
comparing him to the rest of the team. It tells us
nobody is faster than Ty.
Discuss how suffixes change word meanings as you
read and spell.
1 do: smaller
'do: highest
We do: smallest,
We do: sharpest, higher,
sharper, softer, tallest,
quickest
shortest, longest, newer,
hardest, shorter

rit

each of the words in context, emphasizing how the
-ER and -EST endings change the word meanings.
loud > louder > loudest

introduction
A verb is an action word, or a word that tells what
someone or something is doing. Jump and yell are
examples of verbs. We use past tense verbs when
we talk about actions that already happened. We
can add the ending -ED to verbs to make them
past tense:
| jump,
| jumped.
| yell,
| yelled.
There are three ways to pronounce the -ED
ending: /d/ /t/ or /ad/. We can figure out how to
pronounce the -ED ending by thinking about the
sound that comes right before it.
If a voiced sound comes before -ED we say /d/ like
in called and tamed.
If an unvoiced sound comes before -ED we say /t/
like in sniffed and helped.
If the sounds /d/ or /t/ comes before -ED, -ED
makes two sounds: /a/ and /d/ and the E
is
pronounced with a schwa, just like the E in -ES.
Listen to how I say the second syllable of these
words: landed, handed, shifted. Did you hear the
faf?
As you read and spell words, emphasize the
different sounds -ED spells.
Articulatory Gesture
The schwa is the most relaxed of all sounds. The
schwa is made right in the middle of your mouth,
with no stretching or tightening of your lips or
tongue. To make the schwa /a/ sound, open
your mouth just a
little bit, like this...(model). Be
sure your voice is on. Unlike all the other vowel
sounds, we don't really stretch out the schwa. We
say it more quickly. Watch me /a/. You try /a/.
Sometimes, a schwa can sound a
little like a short
u /G/ or a short i /i/, but it's actually more relaxed
than either of those. Try again /a/.
Add '-ed' under the /d/ and /t/ picture cards. Refer
to 'e' under the Ll picture card.

-ING is an ending we can add to verbs to change
their tense. -ING is pronounced /i//n/ like at the
end of the word sing. Can you hear the /1/ and the
Inf?
We add the -ING ending to verbs when we are
talking about actions that continuously happen
in the present or past. When a verb has the -ING
ending, a helping verb comes before it:
lam going. He is going. We are going.
She was going. They were going.
We can break apart words with the -ING ending
to make them easier to read. Display the word
jumping. To read this word, first | find the -ING
ending and cover it up. | read the base word: jump,
then uncover and read the ending: ing. Last ! blend
them together: jumping.

during reading practice as needed.

| Introduction
Morphemes are
2
word parts that « change a word's
meaning. A suffix is
a morpheme we add to the
end of a word. We know the suffixes -S, -ES, -ER,
and -EST. Today we will learn a new suffix: -LY.
-LY is pronounced /lé/ with the Y making the long
E sound, like in the word slowly.
| Continued
fi 9m previous
column.
-LY means like or manner of. Words that end in
-LY are usually adverbs. Adverbs are words that
describe actions or things that are happening.
For example in the sentence "Tom drives slowly,"
slowly tells us how Tom drives. In the sentence
"Shane screams loudly," loudly describes how
Shane screams.

#### Raw script
```
air, are, ear /air/: The graphemes AIR, ARE, and EAR spell /air/
Instructional Notes
The pronunciation of 'air' varies based on dialect.
In some regions, it is pronounced as long /a/
followed by /r/, and in others, it is an r-controlled
vowel sound.
The /air/ sound produces several homophones
which can be confusing for students, especially
in spelling. If students are having trouble
distinguishing between the different spellings of
/air/ review these words in context and incorporate
vocabulary practice. Examples of /air/ homophones
include fair/fare, bear/bare, pear/pair, stair/stare,
and hair/hare.
1: Phonological Awareness
Blend

eigh /&/: EIGH can spell /G/ like in eight. EIGH can

We have learned that the letter C can spell /s/ when

have also learned that the letter G can spell /j/ when

Today we will learn another way that C can spell /s/
and G can spell /j/ in words. We know that the vowels
are A, E, |, O, U, and sometimes Y. Of these vowels, E,
|, and Y are called "signal vowels"
These "signal vowels" signal, or tell us, that
something will be different in a word.

=SION, -tion: -SION and -TION are final stable syllables
Instructional Notes
The Si and TI in -SION and -TION are Latin spellings
of /sh/. The SI in -SION is often pronounced /zh/ (e.g.,
vision), but -TION is never pronounced this way.

introduction
Syllables are words or word parts with one vowel
sound. Learning about syllables helps us read and
spell.
Continued from previous column...
Final stable syllables always come at the end of a
word. We know one final stable syllable, -LE, as in
apple and puzzle. Two more final stable syllables
are -SION and -TION.
-sion: -SION can be pronounced two ways. When
-SION follows a vowel (including r-controlled
vowels), it is usually pronounced /zhan/ like in the
words vision and diversion. When -SION follows a
consonant letter, it is usually pronounced /shan/
like in the words passion and extension.
-tion: -TION can also be pronounced /shan/ like in
the word fiction. -TION is sometimes pronounced
/chan/ with TI making the /ch/ sound like in the
word question.
We can break big words apart into syllables to
help us read and spell. Display the word rotation. To
read this word

syllables then blend them together.

lesson_id: 121

### Steps found (brief)

- Step 32: 2 ESSE

#### Raw script
```
LJ
=@r, =-Or, "3st: The suffixes -ER,-OR, and -IST mean some(one/thing) that does an action
Instructional Notes
The focus of this lesson is on the meaning of the
suffixes and their role in changing the part of
speech. The concepts of nouns and verbs should be
addressed separately, as needed.
In Lesson 100, students learned about the suffix
-ER when used with adjectives to mean "more
than" (e.g., stronger). In this lesson, -ER is used to
change verbs to nouns (e.g., teach > teacher).
1: Phonological Awareness
Segment
far] /t/ (art)
dentist (den-tist)
li] {s/ [t/ (st)
den (/d/ /&/ /n/)
art-ist (artist)
/s/ [a] {lf (sail)
Jer] Jz] (ors)
sail-ors (sailors)
2: Visual Drill
tist (/t/ /1/ /s/ /t/)
binder (bind-er)
bin (/b/ /1/ /n/ /d/)
er (/er/)
3: Auditory Drill
Phonemes
/8/ (e, y), Al Gi y),
Graphemes
y (/y/, [if /e/), age (/i/),
9 (/9/, fi/), ch (/ch/, /sh/,
/i/ (dge, g, j), Jeh/ (ch,
/k/), tch (/ch/), -ed (/t/,
/d/, fed/), ¢ (/k/, /s/),
a (/4/, /G/),
i (/1/ fif),
0 (/5/, /6/), e (/8/ /8/),

(/n/), wh (/w/),
ph (/f/), th (/th/ /th/),

4: Blending Drill
tch), /n/ (ng), /t/ (t, -ed),
/d/ (d, -ed), /z/ (s, z, zz),
lif Gi, 9), /s/ (S, ss, ©)
Use word chains from previous lessons to review
blending with previously taught concepts. Provide
ample practice with a variety of vowel patterns.
5: New Concept
Introduction
Remember, a morpheme is a word part that
changes a word's meaning. A suffix is
a morpheme
that comes at the end of a word. Today we will
learn about three new suffixes: -ER, -OR, and -IST.
Continued from previous column...
-er: We already know that when we add the suffix
-ER to adjectives, it means more than like in the
word bigger.
When we add the suffix -ER to verbs, or action
words, it makes the verb become a noun meaning
one that does. Teach is a verb. Adding -ER to teach
makes teacher. A teacher is a person who teaches.
Print is a verb. Adding -ER makes printer. A printer
is something that prints.
=or, -ist: -OR and -IST are also used to make verbs
into nouns. -OR is pronounced /er/ as in sailor.
-IST is pronounced /ist/ as in cartoonist. A sailor is
someone who sails. A cartoonist is someone who
draws cartoons.
Discuss how suffixes impact word meanings as you
read and spell.
I do: player, actor,
chemist
We do: trainer, fryer,
keeper, helper, sailor,
visitor, dentist, artist,
florist, cartoonist
I do: teacher, sailor,
cyclist
We do: farmer, heater,
inventor, sculptor,
biologist, cartoonist

definition for each word. See lesson slide deck for
graphic organizer.

Introduction
Suffixes are added to the end of a word to change
the word's meaning. Today we will learn a new
suffix: -NESS.
-NESS is usually pronounced /nas/ with the E
making a schwa sound as in kindness. -NESS
means state of or the way something is. -NESS is
usually added to adjectives to make them nouns.
Kindness means the state of being kind.

Continued from previous column...
/able/ with the A or i making a schwa sound as in
enjoyable and flexible. -ABLE and -IBLE mean able
or can do.
If you enjoy something it means you like it,
enjoyable means something is able to be enjoyed
or liked. Flex means move or bend, flexible means
able to move or bend.
When adding -ABLE or -IBLE to a word that ends
with a silent E, we drop the E before adding the
suffix: observe > observable
Most of the time when we use the suffix -IBLE,
it's added to a root that can't stand alone, like in
possible or edible. For now, we'll focus on whole
words that -IBLE can be added to.
Ido: convertible

Uuni-, bi-, tri-: The prefix UNI- means one, BI- means two, and TRI- means three
Instructional Notes
Be sure students do not confuse the prefixes UNI-
and UN-. For example, in the word unimpressed,
the prefix being used is UN- meaning not, not UNI-
meaning one.
Be sure students do not confuse the prefixes
BI- and BIO-. For example, in the word biology,
the prefix being used is BIO- meaning life, not BI-
meaning two.
1: Phonological Awareness

5: New Concept
Introduction
Ba
Prefixes are added to the beginning of a word to
change the word's meaning. Today we will learn
three new prefixes: UNI-, BI-, and TRI-.
uni-: UNI- means one. The U in UNI- makes the long
/ya/ sound and the | makes the schwa sound /a/, as
in uniform.
bi-: BI- means two. The
| in BI- makes the long /i/
sound, as in bisect.
i
inued fr om previous column.
tri-: TRI- means three. The | in TRI- usually makes
the long /i/ sound, as in triangle but sometimes
makes the short /1/ sound, as in triple.
We can remember what these prefixes mean by
adding each prefix to the base cycle. A unicycle
has one wheel, a bicycle has two wheels, and a
tricycle has three wheels.
| do: unicycle, bicycle,
tricycle

rom previous column.
Morphemes are word parts that change what a
word means. Suffixes and prefixes are types of
morphemes. Let's review the suffixes and prefixes
we have been learning.
-er, -or, -ist: -ER, -OR, and -IST mean "one that" as
in the words teacher, sailor, and artist.
-ish: -ISH means "somewhat like" as in sluggish.
y: -Y means "full of or described as." -Y changes
nouns to adjectives as in the word cloudy.
"ness: -NESS means "state of." -NESS changes
adjectives to nouns as in the word kindness.
"ment: -MENT means "result, act, or state of"
-MENT changes verbs to nouns as in the word
entertainment.
~able, ible: -ABLE and -IBLE mean "able to." They
make words adjectives as in the word breakable.
uni-: UNI- means one as in uniform; BI- means two
as in bicycle; TRI- means three as in triangle.

#### Raw script

| ear /ear/
ear /ear
$ The grapheme EAR can spell /ear/
Instructional Notes
The pronunciation of EAR varies based on dialect.
In some regions, it is pronounced as long /é/
followed by /r/, and in others, it is an r-controlled
vowel sound.
The most common sound for EAR is /ear/. When
students see EAR in text, encourage them to
try decoding with the sound /ear/ first and then
consider the context to determine whether this
makes sense, If it doesn't work or they aren't sure,
try /air/.
1: Phonological Awareness

#### Raw script
```
| x /ks/
x /ks/: The grapheme X
spells /ks/
Instructional Notes
5: New Concept
The letter X is unusual because it is a single letter
that represents the combination of two phonemes:
/k/ and /s/. However, you will notice that this lesson
has two different notations for the sound of x: /ks/
and /k/ /s/. This is intentional.
Blending skills are used for decoding. When
students are decoding SIX, and they see the letter
X, they need to say /ks/ and blend this combination
with the other sounds. So, for the phoneme
blending and decoding activities, pronounce the
/ks/ together.
Segmenting skills are used for encoding. When
students are segmenting the phonemes in a
spoken word with /k/ and /s/ at the end, they must
determine how it will be spelled (e.g., six vs. picks).
So, for the phoneme segmenting and spelling tasks,
pronounce /k/ and /s/ separately.
1: Phonemic Awareness
| Blend
| Segment

5: New Concept
introduction
Today we are going to review the short vowels A, I,
and O. Remember, our mouth muscles are looser,
or more relaxed, when we make short vowel
sounds: /G/, /i/, /6/.
a /é&/: The short vowel A spells /G/ like in the
beginning of the word apple. A can come in the
middle of a word, such as the words cat and map
or at the beginning of a word, such as the words at
and as.
Continued from pr evious column.
am, an: When A comes before the consonants M
and N, the sound changes, like in the words am,
jam, an, and can.
i /i/: The short vowel
I spells /i/ like in the
beginning of the word itch.
| can come in the
middle of a word, such as the words lip and him,
or at the beginning of a word, such as the words in
and is.
o /8/: The short vowel O spells /6/ like in the
beginning of the word octopus. O can come in the
middle of a word, such as the words hot and mop,
or at the beginning of a word, such as the words
on and ox.

The short vowel E spells /&/ like in the beginning of

Continued from previous column.
The vowel sound in closed syllables is short.
Remember, the short vowel sounds are /G/, /é/,
[i], [6], /4/. An example of a closed syllable is sun.
In the syllable sun, the vowel, U, is followed by one
consonant, N, so the vowel sound is short.
We can combine two closed syllables to make
longer words. When these longer words are made
up of two smaller words, we call them compound
words. Let's practice reading compound words.
See lesson slide deck for visual aids to practice the
words sunset and stepdad.
1.
The first part of this word is sun (show card).
2.
The next part of this word is set (show card).
3.
If you put them together (slide cards together),
you get the compound word sunset. What is
the word? (sunset).
4. The compound word sunset has two smaller
words that are closed syllables, sun and set.
When reading compound words with two closed
syllables, first we read the first word or syllable
(step). Then, we read the second word or syllable
(dad). Last, we blend the two words or syllables
together to read the word (stepdad). Continue
modeling with "I do" words during reading practice
{e.g., hot-dog, hotdog).

breath. Your chin will usually drop slightly as you
say each syllable in a word.
bulldog, suntan, sandiot,

| Closed/Closed
Closed/Closed: Reading and spelling words with two closed syllables
Instructional Notes
When reading and spelling multisyllabic words,
reinforce the connection between syllable type
and vowel sound: open syllables have a long vowel
sound, closed syllables have a short vowel sound.
The schwa sound (/e/) can be represented by any
vowel fetter in an unstressed syllable.
1: Phonological Awareness
Segment

5: New Concept
Introduction
We have been learning about closed syllables.
Closed syllables have one vowel that makes a
short sound.
Continued from previous column..
When we put closed syllables together, we can
make longer words. We have learned about
compound words that are made up of two smaller
words, like the word sunset. Let's practice reading
longer words that are not compound words. See
lesson slide deck for visual aids.
1.
The first part of this word is den (show card).
2.
The next part of this word is tist (show card).
3.
If you put them together (slide cards together),
you get the word dentist. What is this word?
(dentist). The word dentist has two closed
syllables, den and tist.
Sometimes, when we read two-syllable words we
hear the schwa sound. We've learned about the
schwa sound /a/. Show me how to say the sound.
So far, we've learned how E can be pronounced as
a schwa in words that end with -ES or -ED, words
like lunches or ended. The schwa can actually be
spelled by any vowel letter: A, E, 1, O, U, and even Y.
In many two-syllable words, one syllable is
stressed or emphasized more than the other. This
means that we say it a little bit louder and hold it
out just a little bit longer. For example, in the word
about, the syllable -bout is louder and longer.
The other syllable is pronounced as a schwa.
The A doesn't represent the /&/ or /@/ sounds. It
represents the /a/. Listen: about.
Watch me read these words. See if you can figure
out which letter is representing the schwa in each
of these words: distant (A), below (E), pencil (I),
ribbon (0), supply (VU), vinyl {Y).
Articulatory Gesture
To make the schwa /a/ sound, open your mouth
just a
little bit, like this...(model). Be sure your
voice is on. Unlike all the other vowel sounds, we
don't really stretch out the schwa. We say it more
quickly. Watch me /a/. You try /a/.
Add 'a', 'e', 'i', 'o', 'u', and 'y' under the /a/ picture
card.
Continued on next page...
